I know it's a joke and all, but hiding things is actually a very good security measure.   Not necessarily by painting the lock in camo, but by hiding where things of value are.   It reminds me of some documentary I saw long ago regarding spy satellites and aerial recon. They had found that an obvious way to figure out which buildings were important were simply to see how many security measures were protecting it. If it was within three rings of fences, barbed wire, mine fields and armed guards, it was quite likely a place of high value.  Using this logic, but in reverse. If you HIDE the treasure or secret rather than try to secure it with a massive (pickable) lock, tall fence, or expensive safe it is less likely to be discovered at all. You can even have that overly secure place as a decoy and hide the real secret somewhere unassuming.  I guess the old-school idea of hiding a safe behind a painting isn't as silly as it might seem (although this is probably so cliche that everyone knows it by now)  You can't steal what you can't find.

I've been picking Master padlocks and some other brands and types for years. The ones that are spring loaded (the lock pops open) are the easiest. The ones that you have to pull down on are a little harder (combination locks are like that). In the first case you just have to hit the lock on the side with a hammer or other heavy object. It often takes several whacks to get it done. If it isn't spring loaded, you need to wrap a cord around the body of the lock and pull on it while you strike it with the hammer. The metal the locks are made of are hard enough that no dents are left in the lock when it's struck by the hammer.

Since lock picking tools have a certain length, wouldn't an effective alternative be a larger lock? So long as the lock itself is hard to break, then your only option would be to have the key or invent a new pick for the lock. Hiding the lock mechanism would also work wonders, though I sorta understand that lock-pickers would simply buy the lock, break it apart, then see what's inside... wait... break it apart? What if... it was simply too hard to break apart? Then hide the mechanism that makes the lock work and make it too deep for current lock-picking tools to reach. That way, the only way to counter it is to buy it yourself and try to invent tools to pick it or get your hands on some internal docs about how it's made and/or how the key works. I guess that's kinda... a lot of work. Probably not worth the effort unless your lock is protecting a bank vault or something.

There is a padlock that was made by a madman not very long ago that was declared unpickable by any means and can only be opened by the key made for it. What made it unpickable? The heavy duty padlock uses a circuit inside that is powed by the key itself when inserted. There are no tumblers to pick, grooves nor anything to turn to open it. The locking mechanism is activated by a servo and is only exposed when the cylindrical key with 8 tiny slots all around is inserted. Once inserted, it automatically unlocks the padlock, nothing to turn. When you remove the key and lock it, that's it. It was taken all over the world to be picked by the best of the best, and all of them failed. Wouldn't you like to take a shot of cracking something like that huh?

I thought that was a good letter. According to Google, David Youn is the president of Master Lock. I don't know what his signature looks like but I bet the letter is genuine. I'm not going to hate on Master Lock. They are not hard to pick, but I don't buy their locks for something that needs high security (obviously). I think Mr. Youn and his company have a targeted use case that they address well. My dad has a Master Lock on his shed that has lasted about 40 years. If anyone was serious about breaking in they would just pry the hasp so strong pick resistance isn't a requirement. The lock does the job to keep neighborhood kids out and to help keep the wind from blowing the doors open. If dad put a $200 lock on the shed, he would be more worried about someone stealing the lock than anything it guards. I put their locks on ammo dry boxes to keep curious nephews and nieces out and on our backyard fence's gate. I think their company offers a cheap solution that is ideal for use cases where an deterrent is warranted but bypassing the lock is not difficult.   Kudos to Master Lock for not taking themselves too seriously. Thanks for sharing. I really enjoy your videos.
